
You are an expert academic assistant. Your task is to provide a comprehensive solution for the following university-level assignment.

Please analyze the assignment description and any supplementary content (files, transcripts) carefully and generate a complete response.

--- ASSIGNMENT DETAILS ---
Assignment Name: R01: SWOT Analysis (inspired by UCSC Experiments video)
Description (cleaned):
For this reading response assignment, we want you to run an opinionated SWOT analysis for Teaching with Generative AI. However, instead of having you immediately launch into generating ideas, we want you to get grounded in concrete examples. When you think about the future of Teaching with Generative AI, we want you to be imagining specific teachers, specific courses, and specific assignments and other activities within those courses.
Begin by watching this 100-minute video of a sequence of short talks presented during the Experiments in AI in Teaching and Learning . All of the presenters in this video are people you might bump into on campus, and they are a good representative of the range of experiments that are already ongoing here. Feel free to watch the video at something other than 1x speed if you are comfortable with doing so.
https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=TTF_1ENAwVs
After you've watched the video, come back here to Canvas to share your thoughts in the form of a SWOT analysis . In a SWOT analysis, you try to map out a strategic landscape (a space of action policies we might adopt) by spreading it over four categories:
Strengths: What does GenAI seem to be good at or help in relation to teaching?
Weaknesses: Where does GenAI fail or cause harm in relation to teaching (even if somewhat speculative)?
Opportunities: What outside development or action could make GenAI more helpful in teaching (e.g. if solved or implemented)?
Threats: What outside factors could block GenAI from having beneficial impacts or lead to harmful effects?
Strengths and Weaknesses should relate to factors we can control by deciding how to implement GenAI in teaching. Meanwhile, Opportunities and Threats should represent things outside of our control (dependent on the actions of other organizations). These might include development of similar technologies with slightly different properties (e.g. How would the development of "something with the capabilities of ChatGPT but no requirement for GPU-like hardware for inference, capable of running on common smartphones" change the landscape?)
Don’t try to make a fair and accurate list of all S/W/O/T items. Instead, give us your 6 strongest ideas (your spiciest takes) in a way that is roughly spread out over the S/W/O/T categories . Where possible, try to include a clickable hyperlink to an outside source of support for your ideas or claims.
--- END OF ASSIGNMENT DETAILS ---

--- SUPPLEMENTARY CONTENT (Files & Transcripts) ---

					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Business planning and analysis technique</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1236090951">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/SWOT_(disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" title="SWOT (disambiguation)">SWOT</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:SWOT_en.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/SWOT_en.svg/250px-SWOT_en.svg.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="281" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/SWOT_en.svg/375px-SWOT_en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/SWOT_en.svg/500px-SWOT_en.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="320" data-file-height="360" /></a><figcaption>The four components of SWOT in a 2&#160;×&#160;2 matrix</figcaption></figure>

</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1129693374" /><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1239400231">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .navbar li a abbr{color:var(--color-base)!important}@media(prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .navbar li a abbr{color:var(--color-base)!important}}@media print{.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:none!important}}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Strategy" title="Template:Strategy"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Strategy" title="Template talk:Strategy"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a href="/wiki/Special:EditPage/Template:Strategy" title="Special:EditPage/Template:Strategy"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Strategic_planning" title="Strategic planning">strategic planning</a> and <a href="/wiki/Strategic_management" title="Strategic management">strategic management</a>, <b>SWOT analysis</b> (also known as the <b>SWOT matrix</b>, <b>TOWS</b>, <b>WOTS</b>, <b><abbr title="Weaknesses, Opportunities, Threats and Strengths Underlying Planning">WOTS-UP</abbr></b>, and <a href="/wiki/Situation_analysis" title="Situation analysis">situational analysis</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> is a <a href="/wiki/Decision-making" title="Decision-making">decision-making</a> technique that identifies the strengths, weaknesses, opportunities, and threats of an <a href="/wiki/Organization" title="Organization">organization</a> or project.
</p><p>SWOT analysis evaluates the strategic position of organizations and is often used in the preliminary stages of decision-making processes<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> to identify internal and external factors that are favorable and unfavorable to achieving goals. Users of a SWOT analysis ask questions to generate answers for each category and identify <a href="/wiki/Competitive_advantage" title="Competitive advantage">competitive advantages</a>.
</p><p>SWOT has been described as a "tried-and-true" tool of strategic analysis,<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> but has also been criticized for limitations such as the static nature of the analysis, the influence of personal biases in identifying key factors, and the overemphasis on external factors, leading to reactive strategies. Consequently, alternative approaches to SWOT have been developed over the years.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Overview">Overview</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=SWOT_analysis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Overview"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The name is an acronym for four components:
</p>
<ul><li><em>Strengths</em>: characteristics of the business or project that give it an advantage over others</li>
<li><em>Weaknesses</em>: characteristics that place the business or project at a disadvantage relative to others</li>
<li><em>Opportunities</em>: elements in the environment that the business or project could exploit to its advantage</li>
<li><em>Threats</em>: elements in the environment that could cause trouble for the business or project</li></ul>
<p>Results of the assessment are often presented in the form of a <a href="/wiki/Table_(information)" title="Table (information)">matrix</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Ansoff1980_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ansoff1980-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Internal_and_external_factors">Internal and external factors</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=SWOT_analysis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Internal and external factors"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Strengths and weaknesses are usually considered internal, while opportunities and threats are usually considered external.<sup id="cite_ref-Minsky2021_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Minsky2021-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The degree to which an organization's internal strengths matches with its external opportunities is known as its <a href="/wiki/Strategic_fit" title="Strategic fit">strategic fit</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Learned1965_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Learned1965-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mintzberg1998_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mintzberg1998-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Internal factors may include:<sup id="cite_ref-CTB_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CTB-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Human resources—staff, volunteers, board members, stakeholders</li>
<li>Physical resources—location, building, equipment, plant</li>
<li>Financial—revenue, grants, investments, other sources of income</li>
<li>Activities and processes—projects, programs, systems</li>
<li>Past experiences—reputation, knowledge</li></ul>
<p>External factors may include:<sup id="cite_ref-CTB_9-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CTB-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Future trends in the organization's field or society at large (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Macroeconomic" class="mw-redirect" title="Macroeconomic">macroeconomics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Technological_change" title="Technological change">technological change</a>)</li>
<li>The economy—local, national, or international</li>
<li>Funding sources—investors, foundations, donors, legislatures</li>
<li>Demographics—changes in the age, race, gender, culture of those in the organization serviceable area</li>
<li>Physical environment—growth of location in which organisation is situated, access to location</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Legislation" title="Legislation">Legislation</a></li>
<li>Local, national, or international events</li></ul>
<p>A number of authors advocate assessing external factors before internal factors.<sup id="cite_ref-Minsky2021_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Minsky2021-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Weihrich1982_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weihrich1982-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Use">Use</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=SWOT_analysis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Use"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>SWOT analysis has been used at different <a href="/wiki/Level_of_analysis" title="Level of analysis">levels of analysis</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Business" title="Business">businesses</a>, <a href="/wiki/Non-profit_organizations" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-profit organizations">non-profit organizations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Government_agency" title="Government agency">governmental units</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Person" title="Person">individuals</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-SWOTlimits_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SWOTlimits-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It is often used alongside other frameworks, such as <a href="/wiki/PEST_analysis" title="PEST analysis">PEST</a>, as a basis for the analysis of internal and environmental factors.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> SWOT analysis may also be used in pre-crisis planning, preventive <a href="/wiki/Crisis_management" title="Crisis management">crisis management</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Viability_study" title="Viability study">viability study</a> recommendation construction.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Strategic_planning">Strategic planning</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=SWOT_analysis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Strategic planning"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>SWOT analysis can be used to build organizational or personal strategy. Steps necessary to execute strategy-oriented analysis involve identifying internal and external factors, selecting and evaluating the most important factors, and identifying relationships between internal and external features.<sup id="cite_ref-Pickton1998_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pickton1998-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> For instance, strong relations between strengths and opportunities can suggest good conditions in the company and allow using an <em>aggressive</em> strategy. On the other hand, strong interactions between weaknesses and threats could be analyzed as a warning to use a <em>defensive</em> strategy.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>One form of SWOT analysis combines each of the four components with another to examine four distinct strategies:<sup id="cite_ref-Weihrich1982_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Weihrich1982-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>WT strategy (mini–mini): Faced with external threats and internal weaknesses, how to minimize both weaknesses and threats?</li>
<li>WO strategy (mini–maxi): Faced with external opportunities and internal weaknesses, how to minimize weaknesses and maximize opportunities?</li>
<li>ST strategy (maxi–mini): Faced with internal strengths and external threats, how to maximize strengths and minimize threats?</li>
<li>SO strategy (maxi–maxi): Faced with external opportunities and internal strengths, how to maximize both opportunities and strengths?</li></ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Matching_and_converting">Matching and converting</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=SWOT_analysis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Matching and converting"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A SWOT analysis can be used to generate matching and converting strategies.<sup id="cite_ref-Piercy1989_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Piercy1989-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Matching refers to seeking <a href="/wiki/Competitive_advantage" title="Competitive advantage">competitive advantage</a> by matching strengths to opportunities. This strategy ensures that an organization leverages its core competencies, resources, and capabilities to capitalize on favorable market conditions, emerging trends, or unmet customer needs. Conversion refers to  converting weaknesses or threats into strengths or opportunities. An example of a conversion strategy is to buy off a threat through collaboration or merger.<sup id="cite_ref-Piercy1989_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Piercy1989-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Marketing">Marketing</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=SWOT_analysis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Marketing"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Marketing_management" title="Marketing management">Marketing management</a></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Marketing_mix" title="Marketing mix">Marketing mix</a></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Competitor_analysis" title="Competitor analysis">competitor analysis</a>, marketers can use SWOT analysis to detail and profile the competitive strengths and weaknesses of each competitor in the market. This process may involve analysing competitors' cost structures, sources of profits, resources and competencies, competitive positioning, <a href="/wiki/Product_differentiation" title="Product differentiation">product differentiation</a>, degree of <a href="/wiki/Vertical_integration" title="Vertical integration">vertical integration</a>, historical responses to industry developments, among other factors. Relevant <a href="/wiki/Marketing_research" title="Marketing research">marketing research</a> methods may include:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Qualitative_marketing_research" title="Qualitative marketing research">Qualitative marketing research</a> such as focus groups</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Quantitative_marketing_research" title="Quantitative marketing research">Quantitative marketing research</a> such as statistical surveys</li>
<li>Experimental techniques such as test markets</li>
<li>Observational techniques such as ethnographic (on-site) observation</li></ul>
<p>Marketing managers may also design and oversee various <a href="/wiki/Market_environment" title="Market environment">environmental scanning</a> and <a href="/wiki/Competitive_intelligence" title="Competitive intelligence">competitive intelligence</a> processes to help identify trends and inform the company's marketing analysis.
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<caption>SWOT analysis of the market position of a small management consultancy with a specialism in human resource management<sup id="cite_ref-Armstrong1990_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Armstrong1990-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</caption>
<tbody><tr>

</th>

</th>

</th></tr>

</td>

</td>

</td>

</td></tr>

</td>

</td>

</td>
<td>Other small consultancies looking to invade the marketplace
</td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="In_community_organizations">In community organizations</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=SWOT_analysis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: In community organizations"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:SWOT_Analysis_ssw_1.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/SWOT_Analysis_ssw_1.png/250px-SWOT_Analysis_ssw_1.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="225" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/SWOT_Analysis_ssw_1.png/500px-SWOT_Analysis_ssw_1.png 1.5x" data-file-width="518" data-file-height="467" /></a><figcaption>An example of a SWOT template that includes cells for strategies, not only assessments</figcaption></figure>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:SWOT_Analysis_ssw_2.png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/SWOT_Analysis_ssw_2.png/250px-SWOT_Analysis_ssw_2.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="233" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/SWOT_Analysis_ssw_2.png/500px-SWOT_Analysis_ssw_2.png 1.5x" data-file-width="559" data-file-height="522" /></a><figcaption>A simple SWOT template</figcaption></figure>
<p>Although the SWOT analysis was originally designed for business and industries, it has been used in <a href="/wiki/Non-governmental_organization" title="Non-governmental organization">non-governmental organisations</a> as a tool for identifying external and internal support to combat internal and external opposition for successful implementation of <a href="/wiki/Social_services" title="Social services">social services</a> and <a href="/wiki/Social_change" title="Social change">social change</a> efforts.<sup id="cite_ref-CTB_9-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CTB-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Understanding particular communities can come from public forums, listening campaigns, and informational interviews and other data collection.<sup id="cite_ref-CTB_9-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CTB-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> SWOT analysis provides direction to the next stages of the <a href="/wiki/Change_management" title="Change management">change process</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Birkenmaier2001_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birkenmaier2001-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It has been used by community organizers and community members to further social justice in the context of social work practice,<sup id="cite_ref-Birkenmaier2001_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Birkenmaier2001-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and can be applied directly to communities served by a specific nonprofit or community organization.<sup id="cite_ref-social_work_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-social_work-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Limitations_and_alternatives">Limitations and alternatives</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=SWOT_analysis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Limitations and alternatives"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>SWOT analysis is intended as a starting point for discussion and not to, in itself, show managers how to achieve a competitive advantage.<sup id="cite_ref-Dess2012_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dess2012-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In a highly-cited 1997 critique, "SWOT Analysis: It's Time for a Product Recall", Terry Hill and Roy Westbrook observed that one among many problems of SWOT analysis as often practiced is that "no-one subsequently used the outputs [of SWOT analysis] within the later stages of the strategy".<sup id="cite_ref-HillWestbrook1997_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HillWestbrook1997-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Hill and Westbrook, among others, also criticized hastily designed SWOT lists.<sup id="cite_ref-HillWestbrook1997_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HillWestbrook1997-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Other limitations of SWOT practice include: preoccupation with a single strength, such as cost control, leading to a neglect of weaknesses, such as product quality;<sup id="cite_ref-Dess2012_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dess2012-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and domination by one or two team members doing the SWOT analysis and devaluing possibly important contributions of other team members.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Many other limitations have been identified.<sup id="cite_ref-Pickton1998_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pickton1998-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Business professors have suggested various ways to remedy the common problems and limitations of SWOT analysis while retaining the SWOT framework.<sup id="cite_ref-SWOTlimits_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SWOTlimits-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Porter's_five_forces"><span id="Porter.27s_five_forces"></span>Porter's five forces</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=SWOT_analysis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Porter&#039;s five forces"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Porter%27s_five_forces_analysis" title="Porter&#39;s five forces analysis">Porter's five forces analysis</a></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Porter%27s_four_corners_model" title="Porter&#39;s four corners model">Porter's four corners model</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Michael_Porter" title="Michael Porter">Michael Porter</a> developed the <a href="/wiki/Porter%27s_five_forces_analysis" title="Porter&#39;s five forces analysis">five forces framework</a> as an alternative to SWOT analyses, which he found lacking in rigor and over-dependent on individual company circumstances.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="SOAR">SOAR</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=SWOT_analysis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: SOAR"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>SOAR (strengths, opportunities, aspirations, and results) is an alternative technique inspired by <a href="/wiki/Appreciative_inquiry" title="Appreciative inquiry">appreciative inquiry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> SOAR has been criticized as having similar limitations as SWOT, such as "the inability to identify the necessary data".<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="SVOR">SVOR</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=SWOT_analysis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: SVOR"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In project management, the alternative to SWOT known by the acronym SVOR (Strengths, Vulnerabilities, Opportunities, and Risks) compares the project elements along two axes: internal and external, and positive and negative.<sup id="cite_ref-Mesly_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mesly-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It takes into account the mathematical link that exists between these various elements, considering also the role of infrastructures. The SVOR table provides an intricate understanding of the elements hypothesized to be at play in a given project:<sup id="cite_ref-Mesly_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mesly-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 9">&#58;&#8202;9&#8202;</span></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable">

<tbody><tr>
<th>Forces</th>
<th>Internal</th>
<th>Mathematical link</th>
<th>External
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><em>Positive</em></td>
<td>Total Forces</td>
<td>Total Forces given constraints = Infrastructures / Opportunities</td>
<td>Opportunities
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><em>Mathematical link</em></td>
<td>Vulnerabilities given constraints = 1 / Total Forces</td>
<td>constant <i>k</i></td>
<td>Opportunities given constraints = 1 / Risks
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><em>Negative</em></td>
<td>Vulnerabilities</td>
<td>Risks given constraints = <i>k</i> / Vulnerabilities</td>
<td>Risks
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Constraints consist of: calendar of tasks and activities, costs, and norms of quality. The "<i>k</i>" constant varies with each project (for example, it may be valued at 1.3).<sup id="cite_ref-Mesly_29-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mesly-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 9">&#58;&#8202;9&#8202;</span></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History">History</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=SWOT_analysis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: History"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In 1965, three colleagues at the Long Range Planning Service (LRPS) of <a href="/wiki/Stanford_Research_Institute" class="mw-redirect" title="Stanford Research Institute">Stanford Research Institute</a>—Robert F. Stewart, Otis J. Benepe, and <a href="/wiki/Arnold_Mitchell" title="Arnold Mitchell">Arnold Mitchell</a>—wrote a technical report titled <i>Formal Planning: The Staff Planner's Role at Start-Up</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Puyt2020_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Puyt2020-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The report described how a person in the role of a company's staff planner would gather information from managers assessing operational issues grouped into four components represented by the acronym SOFT: the "satisfactory" in present operations, "opportunities" in future operations, "faults" in present operations, and "threats" to future operations.<sup id="cite_ref-Puyt2020_30-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Puyt2020-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Stewart et al. focused on internal operational assessment and divided the four components into <em>present</em> (satisfactory and fault) and <em>future</em> (opportunity and threat),<sup id="cite_ref-Puyt2020_30-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Puyt2020-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and not, as would later become common in SWOT analysis, into <em>internal</em> (strengths and weaknesses) and <em>external</em> (opportunities and threats).<sup id="cite_ref-Learned1965_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Learned1965-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Also in 1965, four colleagues at the <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Graduate_School_of_Business_Administration" class="mw-redirect" title="Harvard Graduate School of Business Administration">Harvard Graduate School of Business Administration</a> (later the Harvard Business School)—Edmund P. Learned, C. Roland Christensen, <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_R._Andrews" title="Kenneth R. Andrews">Kenneth R. Andrews</a>, and William D. Guth—published the first of many editions of the textbook <i>Business Policy: Text and Cases</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Learned1965_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Learned1965-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> (<em>Business policy</em> was a term then current for what has come to be called strategic management.<sup id="cite_ref-Browne1999_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Browne1999-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>) The first chapter of the textbook stated, without using the acronym, the four components of SWOT and their division into internal and external appraisal:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1244412712">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 32px}.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;margin-top:0}@media(min-width:500px){.mw-parser-output .templatequotecite{padding-left:1.6em}}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Deciding what strategy should be is, at least ideally, a rational undertaking. Its principal subactivities include identifying opportunities and threats in the company's environment and attaching some estimate of risk to the discernible alternatives. Before a choice can be made, the company's strengths and weaknesses must be appraised.<sup id="cite_ref-Learned1965_6-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Learned1965-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>Looking back from three decades later, in the book <i>Strategy Safari</i> (1998), management scholar <a href="/wiki/Henry_Mintzberg" title="Henry Mintzberg">Henry Mintzberg</a> and colleagues said that <i>Business Policy: Text and Cases</i> "quickly became the most popular classroom book in the field", widely diffusing its authors' ideas, which Mintzberg et al. called the "design school" model (in contrast to nine other schools that they identified) of strategic management, "with its famous notion of SWOT" emphasizing assessment of a company's internal and external situations.<sup id="cite_ref-Mintzberg1998_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mintzberg1998-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mintzberg1990_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mintzberg1990-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Browne1999_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Browne1999-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, the textbook contains neither a 2&#160;×&#160;2 SWOT matrix nor any detailed procedure for doing a SWOT assessment.<sup id="cite_ref-Learned1965_6-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Learned1965-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <i>Strategy Safari</i> and other books identified <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_R._Andrews" title="Kenneth R. Andrews">Kenneth R. Andrews</a> as the co-author of <i>Business Policy: Text and Cases</i> who was responsible for writing the theoretical part of the book containing the SWOT components.<sup id="cite_ref-Mintzberg1998_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mintzberg1998-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> More generally, Mintzberg et al. attributed some conceptual influences on what they called the "design school" (of which they were strongly critical) to earlier books by <a href="/wiki/Philip_Selznick" title="Philip Selznick">Philip Selznick</a> (<i>Leadership in Administration</i>, 1957) and <a href="/wiki/Alfred_D._Chandler_Jr." title="Alfred D. Chandler Jr.">Alfred D. Chandler Jr.</a> (<i>Strategy and Structure</i>, 1962),<sup id="cite_ref-Mintzberg1998_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mintzberg1998-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> with other possible influences going back to the <a href="/wiki/McKinsey" class="mw-redirect" title="McKinsey">McKinsey</a> consulting firm in the 1930s.<sup id="cite_ref-Mintzberg1990_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mintzberg1990-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>However, a 2023 history of SWOT by Richard W. Puyt and colleagues criticized Mintzberg's "vilification of SWOT" and Mintzberg's apparently poor knowledge of the LRPS at Stanford.<sup id="cite_ref-Puyt2023_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Puyt2023-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Puyt et al. considered the LRPS to be the originator of SWOT (via SOFT) and said that the claim of Mintzberg and others that SWOT was invented at, or disseminated by, Harvard Business School is an "academic urban legend".<sup id="cite_ref-Puyt2023_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Puyt2023-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>By the end of the 1960s, the four components of SWOT (without using the acronym) had appeared in other publications on strategic planning by various authors,<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and by 1972 the acronym had appeared in the title of a journal article by Norman Stait, a <a href="/wiki/Management_consulting" title="Management consulting">management consultant</a> at the British firm Urwick, Orr and Partners.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By 1973, the acronym was well-known enough that accountant William W. Fea, in a published lecture, mentioned "the mnemonic, familiar to students, of S.W.O.T., namely strengths, weaknesses, opportunities, threats".<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Early examples of a 2&#160;×&#160;2 SWOT matrix are found in John Argenti's book <i>Systematic Corporate Planning</i> (1974)<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and in a 1980 article by management professor <a href="/wiki/Igor_Ansoff" title="Igor Ansoff">Igor Ansoff</a> (but Ansoff used the acronym T/O/S/W instead of SWOT).<sup id="cite_ref-Ansoff1980_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ansoff1980-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In the 1960s Ansoff had worked with the LRPS, where the SOFT approach originated.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=SWOT_analysis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: In popular culture"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<ul><li>Television: In the 2015 <i><a href="/wiki/Silicon_Valley_(TV_series)" title="Silicon Valley (TV series)">Silicon Valley</a></i> episode "Homicide" (Season 2, Episode 6), Jared Dunn (<a href="/wiki/Zach_Woods" title="Zach Woods">Zach Woods</a>) introduces the Pied Piper team to SWOT analysis. Later in that episode Dinesh (<a href="/wiki/Kumail_Nanjiani" title="Kumail Nanjiani">Kumail Nanjiani</a>) and Gilfoyle (<a href="/wiki/Martin_Starr" title="Martin Starr">Martin Starr</a>) employ the method when deciding whether or not to inform a stunt driver that the calculations for his upcoming jump were performed incorrectly.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></li></ul>

<li id="cite_note-2"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-2">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222" /><cite id="CITEREFSilva2005" class="citation book cs1">Silva, Carlos Nunes (2005). <span class="id-lock-registration" title="Free registration required"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/encyclopediaofci0000unse_o0h4/page/444">"SWOT analysis"</a></span>. In Caves, Roger W. (ed.). <i>Encyclopedia of the city</i>. Abingdon; New York: <a href="/wiki/Routledge" title="Routledge">Routledge</a>. pp.&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/encyclopediaofci0000unse_o0h4/page/444">444–445</a>. <a href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.4324%2F9780203484234">10.4324/9780203484234</a>. <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0415862875" title="Special:BookSources/978-0415862875"><bdi>978-0415862875</bdi></a>. <a href="/wiki/OCLC_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="OCLC (identifier)">OCLC</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://search.worldcat.org/oclc/55948158">55948158</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=SWOT+analysis&amp;rft.btitle=Encyclopedia+of+the+city&amp;rft.place=Abingdon%3B+New+York&amp;rft.pages=444-445&amp;rft.pub=Routledge&amp;rft.date=2005&amp;rft_id=info%3Aoclcnum%2F55948158&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.4324%2F9780203484234&amp;rft.isbn=978-0415862875&amp;rft.aulast=Silva&amp;rft.aufirst=Carlos+Nunes&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Farchive.org%2Fdetails%2Fencyclopediaofci0000unse_o0h4%2Fpage%2F444&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASWOT+analysis" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Examples of the "tried-and-true" trope:
<ul><li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222" /><cite id="CITEREFSevier2001" class="citation book cs1">Sevier, Robert A. (2001). <span class="id-lock-registration" title="Free registration required"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/thinkingoutsideb0000sevi/page/46">"Not SWOT, but OTSW"</a></span>. <i>Thinking outside the box: some (fairly) radical thoughts on how colleges and universities should think, act, and communicate in a very busy marketplace</i>. Hiawatha, Iowa: Strategy Pub. p.&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/thinkingoutsideb0000sevi/page/46">46</a>. <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0971059705" title="Special:BookSources/0971059705"><bdi>0971059705</bdi></a>. <a href="/wiki/OCLC_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="OCLC (identifier)">OCLC</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://search.worldcat.org/oclc/48165005">48165005</a>. <q>Few people realize that there is an inherent danger in conducting a situational analysis using the old tried and true SWOT. The danger is this: When you look inside the organization first, you create a set of glasses through which you will look at the world. In doing so, you are highly likely to overlook significant opportunities and threats.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Not+SWOT%2C+but+OTSW&amp;rft.btitle=Thinking+outside+the+box%3A+some+%28fairly%29+radical+thoughts+on+how+colleges+and+universities+should+think%2C+act%2C+and+communicate+in+a+very+busy+marketplace&amp;rft.place=Hiawatha%2C+Iowa&amp;rft.pages=46&amp;rft.pub=Strategy+Pub.&amp;rft.date=2001&amp;rft_id=info%3Aoclcnum%2F48165005&amp;rft.isbn=0971059705&amp;rft.aulast=Sevier&amp;rft.aufirst=Robert+A.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Farchive.org%2Fdetails%2Fthinkingoutsideb0000sevi%2Fpage%2F46&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASWOT+analysis" class="Z3988"></span> See also <a href="#CITEREFMinskyAron2021">Minsky &amp; Aron 2021</a>.</li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222" /><cite id="CITEREFStaples2004" class="citation book cs1">Staples, Lee (2004). <i>Roots to power: a manual for grassroots organizing</i> (2nd&#160;ed.). Westport, Conn.: <a href="/wiki/Praeger_Publishing" class="mw-redirect" title="Praeger Publishing">Praeger Publishing</a>. p.&#160;136. <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0275969975" title="Special:BookSources/0275969975"><bdi>0275969975</bdi></a>. <a href="/wiki/OCLC_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="OCLC (identifier)">OCLC</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://search.worldcat.org/oclc/56085984">56085984</a>. <q>The tried and true SWOT Assessment examines positive and negative factors as does a Force Field Analysis, but a SWOT has a particular focus on the upsides and downsides for the action group itself.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Roots+to+power%3A+a+manual+for+grassroots+organizing&amp;rft.place=Westport%2C+Conn.&amp;rft.pages=136&amp;rft.edition=2nd&amp;rft.pub=Praeger+Publishing&amp;rft.date=2004&amp;rft_id=info%3Aoclcnum%2F56085984&amp;rft.isbn=0275969975&amp;rft.aulast=Staples&amp;rft.aufirst=Lee&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASWOT+analysis" class="Z3988"></span></li>
<li><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222" /><cite id="CITEREFLambertParker2006" class="citation book cs1">Lambert, Ron; Parker, Tom (2006). <span class="id-lock-registration" title="Free registration required"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/isthatyourhandin00lamb/page/132"><i>Is that your hand in my pocket?: the sales professional's guide to negotiating</i></a></span>. Nashville: Nelson Business. p.&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/isthatyourhandin00lamb/page/132">132</a>. <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0785218777" title="Special:BookSources/0785218777"><bdi>0785218777</bdi></a>. <a href="/wiki/OCLC_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="OCLC (identifier)">OCLC</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://search.worldcat.org/oclc/63125604">63125604</a>. <q>Before you as a salesperson can develop a strategy, you have to assess the situation. We recommend the tried-and-true SWOT analysis. You start by taking a look at your Strengths and Weaknesses, your Opportunities and any Threats. Then you do exactly the same thing from the perspective of each of your competitors.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Is+that+your+hand+in+my+pocket%3F%3A+the+sales+professional%27s+guide+to+negotiating&amp;rft.place=Nashville&amp;rft.pages=132&amp;rft.pub=Nelson+Business&amp;rft.date=2006&amp;rft_id=info%3Aoclcnum%2F63125604&amp;rft.isbn=0785218777&amp;rft.aulast=Lambert&amp;rft.aufirst=Ron&amp;rft.au=Parker%2C+Tom&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Farchive.org%2Fdetails%2Fisthatyourhandin00lamb%2Fpage%2F132&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASWOT+analysis" class="Z3988"></span></li></ul>
</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-Ansoff1980-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Ansoff1980_4-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Ansoff1980_4-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222" /><cite id="CITEREFAnsoff1980" class="citation journal cs1"><a href="/wiki/H._Igor_Ansoff" class="mw-redirect" title="H. Igor Ansoff">Ansoff, H. Igor</a> (April 1980). <li id="cite_note-Minsky2021-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Minsky2021_5-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Minsky2021_5-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222" /><cite id="CITEREFMinskyAron2021" class="citation web cs1">Minsky, Laurence; Aron, David (23 February 2021). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://hbr.org/2021/02/are-you-doing-the-swot-analysis-backwards">"Are you doing the SWOT analysis backwards?"</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Harvard_Business_Review" title="Harvard Business Review">Harvard Business Review</a></i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">7 November</span> 2021</span>. <q>The results of a SWOT analysis can be (and almost always are) presented simply as a 2&#160;×&#160;2 grid, with one dimension representing the internal versus external factors, and the other depicting positive versus negative valence.&#160;... To improve the inventory collection, you should start with the external factors, then turn your attention to the firm's internal ones.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Harvard+Business+Review&amp;rft.atitle=Are+you+doing+the+SWOT+analysis+backwards%3F&amp;rft.date=2021-02-23&amp;rft.aulast=Minsky&amp;rft.aufirst=Laurence&amp;rft.au=Aron%2C+David&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fhbr.org%2F2021%2F02%2Fare-you-doing-the-swot-analysis-backwards&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASWOT+analysis" class="Z3988"></span> See also <a href="#CITEREFSevier2001">Sevier 2001</a>.</span>

<li id="cite_note-Learned1965-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Learned1965_6-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Learned1965_6-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Learned1965_6-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Learned1965_6-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Learned1965_6-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222" /><cite id="CITEREFLearnedChristensenAndrewsGuth1965" class="citation book cs1">Learned, Edmund Philip; Christensen, C. Roland; Andrews, Kenneth R.; Guth, William D. (1965). <span class="id-lock-registration" title="Free registration required"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/businesspolicyte0000lear/page/20"><i>Business policy: text and cases</i></a></span> (1st&#160;ed.). Irwin, Inc. p.&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/businesspolicyte0000lear/page/20">20</a>. <a href="/wiki/OCLC_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="OCLC (identifier)">OCLC</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://search.worldcat.org/oclc/680327">680327</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Business+policy%3A+text+and+cases&amp;rft.place=Homewood%2C+Illinois&amp;rft.pages=20&amp;rft.edition=1st&amp;rft.pub=Richard+D.+Irwin%2C+Inc.&amp;rft.date=1965&amp;rft_id=info%3Aoclcnum%2F680327&amp;rft.aulast=Learned&amp;rft.aufirst=Edmund+Philip&amp;rft.au=Christensen%2C+C.+Roland&amp;rft.au=Andrews%2C+Kenneth+R.&amp;rft.au=Guth%2C+William+D.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Farchive.org%2Fdetails%2Fbusinesspolicyte0000lear%2Fpage%2F20&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASWOT+analysis" class="Z3988"></span> (See also <a href="#CITEREFAndrews1971">Andrews 1971</a>, p.&#160;37.) Many publications cite this textbook as an early statement of the ideas behind SWOT, although it contains neither a 2&#160;×&#160;2 matrix nor any detailed procedure for doing a SWOT assessment; for example, <a href="/wiki/Robert_S._Kaplan" title="Robert S. Kaplan">Robert S. Kaplan</a> and <a href="/wiki/David_P._Norton" title="David P. Norton">David P. Norton</a> called this textbook "one of the early SWOT references", in: <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222" /><cite id="CITEREFKaplanNorton2008" class="citation book cs1">Kaplan, Robert S.; Norton, David P. Boston, MA: <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Business_Press" class="mw-redirect" title="Harvard Business Press">Harvard Business Press</a>. p.&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/executionpremium00kapl/page/67">67</a>. <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781422121160" title="Special:BookSources/9781422121160"><bdi>9781422121160</bdi></a>. <a href="/wiki/OCLC_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="OCLC (identifier)">OCLC</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://search.worldcat.org/oclc/227277585">227277585</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+execution+premium%3A+linking+strategy+to+operations+for+competitive+advantage&amp;rft.place=Boston%2C+MA&amp;rft.pages=67&amp;rft.pub=Harvard+Business+Press&amp;rft.date=2008&amp;rft_id=info%3Aoclcnum%2F227277585&amp;rft.isbn=9781422121160&amp;rft.aulast=Kaplan&amp;rft.aufirst=Robert+S.&amp;rft.au=Norton%2C+David+P.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Farchive.org%2Fdetails%2Fexecutionpremium00kapl&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASWOT+analysis" class="Z3988"></span></span>

class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1002%2Fsmj.4250110302">10.1002/smj.4250110302</a></span>. <a href="/wiki/JSTOR_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="JSTOR (identifier)">JSTOR</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/stable/2486485">2486485</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Strategic+Management+Journal&amp;rft.atitle=The+design+school%3A+reconsidering+the+basic+premises+of+strategic+management&amp;rft.volume=11&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.pages=171-195&amp;rft.date=1990-03&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1002%2Fsmj.4250110302&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.jstor.org%2Fstable%2F2486485%23id-name%3DJSTOR&amp;rft.aulast=Mintzberg&amp;rft.aufirst=Henry&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fdoi.org%2F10.1002%252Fsmj.4250110302&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASWOT+analysis" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-33"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-33">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222" /><cite id="CITEREFKiechel2010" class="citation book cs1"><a href="/wiki/Walter_Kiechel" title="Walter Kiechel">Kiechel, Walter</a> (2010). <span class="id-lock-registration" title="Free registration required"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/lordsofstrategys0000kiec/page/121"><i>The lords of strategy: the secret intellectual history of the new corporate world</i></a></span>. Boston, MA: <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Business_Press" class="mw-redirect" title="Harvard Business Press">Harvard Business Press</a>. p.&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/lordsofstrategys0000kiec/page/121">121</a>. <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781591397823" title="Special:BookSources/9781591397823"><bdi>9781591397823</bdi></a>. <a href="/wiki/OCLC_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="OCLC (identifier)">OCLC</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://search.worldcat.org/oclc/259247279">259247279</a>. <q>What Andrews and his colleagues in the Business Policy course resolutely refused to do—and the main reason his ideas largely disappear from the subsequent history of strategy—was to agree that there were standard frameworks or constructs that could be applied to analyzing a business and its competitive situation. Oh, they might allow one, perhaps because they had helped develop it: so-called SWOT analysis, which called for looking at the strengths, weaknesses, opportunities, and threats besetting an enterprise.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=The+lords+of+strategy%3A+the+secret+intellectual+history+of+the+new+corporate+world&amp;rft.place=Boston%2C+MA&amp;rft.pages=121&amp;rft.pub=Harvard+Business+Press&amp;rft.date=2010&amp;rft_id=info%3Aoclcnum%2F259247279&amp;rft.isbn=9781591397823&amp;rft.aulast=Kiechel&amp;rft.aufirst=Walter&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Farchive.org%2Fdetails%2Flordsofstrategys0000kiec%2Fpage%2F121&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASWOT+analysis" class="Z3988"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-34"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-34">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFHillWestbrook1997">Hill &amp; Westbrook 1997</a>, p.&#160;47: "The work of Kenneth Andrews has been especially influential in popularizing the idea that good strategy means ensuring a fit between the external situation a firm faces (threats and opportunities) and its own internal qualities or characteristics (strengths and weaknesses)."</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-35"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-35">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222" /><cite id="CITEREFMcKinsey1932" class="citation book cs1 cs1-prop-long-vol"><a href="/wiki/James_O._McKinsey" title="James O. McKinsey">McKinsey, James Oscar</a> (1932). <i>Adjusting policies to meet changing conditions</i>. General management series. Vol.&#160;G.M. 116. New York: <a href="/wiki/American_Management_Association" title="American Management Association">American Management Association</a>. <a href="/wiki/OCLC_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="OCLC (identifier)">OCLC</a>&#160;<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://search.worldcat.org/oclc/10865820">10865820</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Adjusting+policies+to+meet+changing+conditions&amp;rft.place=New+York&amp;rft.series=General+management+series&amp;rft.pub=American+Management+Association&amp;rft.date=1932&amp;rft_id=info%3Aoclcnum%2F10865820&amp;rft.aulast=McKinsey&amp;rft.aufirst=James+Oscar&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ASWOT+analysis" class="Z3988"></span> Presented at the AMA General Management Conference held in New York, May 3, 1932.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Puyt2023-36"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Puyt2023_36-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Puyt2023_36-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222" /><cite id="CITEREFPuytLieWilderom2023" class="citation journal cs1">Puyt, Richard W.; Lie, Finn Birger; Wilderom, Celeste P. <div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Further_reading">Further reading</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=SWOT_analysis&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Further reading"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>SWOT analysis is described in very many publications. A few examples of books that describe SWOT analysis and are widely held by <a href="/wiki/WorldCat" title="WorldCat">WorldCat</a> member libraries and available in the <a href="/wiki/Internet_Archive" title="Internet Archive">Internet Archive</a> are:
